class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span><span \'code\'">o&#650;</span></span>/</a></span></span>) (denoted by the Greek letter <a href="/wiki/Nu_(letter)" title="Nu (letter)">&#957;</a>) is a <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermion</a> (an <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particle</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin-1/2" title="Spin-1/2">half-integer spin</a>) that interacts only via the <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak subatomic force</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Close_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Close-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jayawardhana_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jayawardhana-3">[3]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> of the neutrino is much smaller than that of the other known elementary particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mertens_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mertens-1">[1]</a></sup> Although only differences of squares of the three mass values are known as of 2016,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Capozzi_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Capozzi-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmological</a> observations imply that the sum of the three masses must be less than one millionth that of the electron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mertens_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mertens-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PDG_mass_sum_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-PDG_mass_sum-5">[5]</a></sup> The neutrino is so named because it is <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electrically</a> neutral and because its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">rest mass</a> is so small (<i><a href="/wiki/List_of_diminutives_by_language#Italian" title="List of diminutives by language">-ino</a></i>) that it was long thought to be zero. The weak force has a very short range, the gravitational interaction is extremely weak, and neutrinos, as <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">leptons</a>, do not participate in the <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">strong interaction</a>. Thus, neutrinos typically pass through normal matter unimpeded and undetected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Close_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Close-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jayawardhana_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jayawardhana-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_interactions" title="Weak interactions">Weak interactions</a> create neutrinos in one of three leptonic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flavor_(particle_physics)" title="Flavor (particle physics)">flavors</a>: <a href="/wiki/Electron_neutrino" title="Electron neutrino">electron neutrinos</a> <span class="nowrap">(<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#957;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span>),</span> <a href="/wiki/Muon_neutrino" title="Muon neutrino">muon neutrinos</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#957;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#956;</sub></span></span>), or <a href="/wiki/Tau_neutrino" title="Tau neutrino">tau neutrinos</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#957;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#964;</sub></span></span>), in association with the corresponding charged lepton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Although neutrinos were long believed to be massless, it is now known that there are three discrete neutrino masses with different tiny values, but they do not correspond uniquely to the three flavors. A neutrino created with a specific flavor is in an associated specific <a href="/wiki/Quantum_superposition" title="Quantum superposition">quantum superposition</a> of all three mass states. As a result, neutrinos <a href="/wiki/Neutrino_oscillation" title="Neutrino oscillation">oscillate</a> between different flavors in flight. For example, an electron neutrino produced in a <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> reaction may interact in a distant detector as a muon or tau neutrino.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GrossmanLipkin_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-GrossmanLipkin-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bilenky_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bilenky-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For each neutrino, there also exists a corresponding <a href="/wiki/Antiparticle" title="Antiparticle">antiparticle</a>, called an <a href="#Antineutrinos"><i>antineutrino</i></a>, which also has half-integer spin and no electric charge. They are distinguished from the neutrinos by having opposite signs of <a href="/wiki/Lepton_number" title="Lepton number">lepton number</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chirality_(physics)" title="Chirality (physics)">chirality</a>. To conserve total lepton number, in nuclear <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>, electron neutrinos appear together with only positrons (anti-electrons) or electron-antineutrinos, and electron antineutrinos with electrons or electron neutrinos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neutrinos_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neutrinos-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conservation_of_lepton_number_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Conservation_of_lepton_number-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Neutrinos are created by various <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decays</a>, including in <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_nuclei" title="Atomic nuclei">atomic nuclei</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">hadrons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reactions</a> such as those that take place in the core of a <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a> or artificially in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_bomb" title="Nuclear bomb">nuclear bombs</a> or <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerators</a>, during a <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a>, in the spin-down of a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron star</a>, or when accelerated particle beams or <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a> strike atoms. The majority of neutrinos in the vicinity of the Earth are from nuclear reactions in the Sun. In the vicinity of the Earth, about 65&#160;billion (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7010650000000000000&#9824;</span>6.5<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>10</sup></span>) <a href="/wiki/Solar_neutrino" title="Solar neutrino">solar neutrinos</a> per second pass through every square centimeter perpendicular to the direction of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Armitage_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Armitage-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-J._Bahcall_et_al._2005_L85&#8211;L88_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-J._Bahcall_et_al._2005_L85&#8211;L88-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For study, neutrinos can be created artificially with nuclear reactors and particle accelerators. There is intense research activity involving neutrinos, with goals that include the determination of the three neutrino mass values, the measurement of the degree of <a href="/wiki/CP_violation" title="CP violation">CP violation</a> in the leptonic sector (leading to <a href="/wiki/Leptogenesis_(physics)" title="Leptogenesis (physics)">leptogenesis</a>); and searches for evidence of physics beyond the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> of <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutrinoless_double_beta_decay" title="Neutrinoless double beta decay">neutrinoless double beta decay</a>, which would be evidence for violation of lepton number conservation. Neutrinos can also be used for <a href="/wiki/Tomography" title="Tomography">tomography</a> of the interior of the earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomography_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tomography-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PINGU_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-PINGU-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label href="#Sources"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Sources</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Artificial"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Artificial</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-31"><a href="#Reactor_neutrinos"><span class="tocnumber">4.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Reactor neutrinos</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-32"><a href="#Accelerator_neutrinos"><span class="tocnumber">4.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Accelerator neutrinos</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-33"><a href="#Nuclear_weapons"><span class="tocnumber">4.1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Nuclear weapons</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34"><a href="#Geologic"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Geologic</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-35"><a href="#Atmospheric"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Atmospheric</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-36"><a href="#Solar"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Solar</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Supernovae"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Supernovae</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#Supernova_remnants"><span class="tocnumber">4.6</span> <span class="toctext">Supernova remnants</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#Big_Bang"><span class="tocnumber">4.7</span> <span class="toctext">Big Bang</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40"><a href="#Detection"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Detection</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#Scientific_interest"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Scientific interest</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Pauli.27s_proposal"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pauli\'s_proposal">Pauli\'s proposal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pauli\'s proposal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The neutrino<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[a]</a></sup> was postulated first by <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Pauli" title="Wolfgang Pauli">Wolfgang Pauli</a> in 1930 to explain how <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> could conserve <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_energy" title="Conservation of energy">energy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_momentum" title="Conservation of momentum">momentum</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_angular_momentum" title="Conservation of angular momentum">angular momentum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a>). In contrast to <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a>, who proposed a statistical version of the conservation laws to explain the observed <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay#Neutrinos_in_beta_decay" title="Beta decay">continuous energy spectra in beta decay</a>, Pauli hypothesized an undetected particle that he called a "neutron", using the same <i>-on</i> ending employed for naming both the <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>. He considered that the new particle was emitted from the nucleus together with the electron or beta particle in the process of beta decay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[b]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/James_Chadwick" title="James Chadwick">James Chadwick</a> discovered a much more massive neutral nuclear particle in 1932 and named it a <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> also, leaving two kinds of particles with the same name. Earlier (in 1930) Pauli had used the term "neutron" for both the neutral particle that conserved energy in beta decay, and a presumed neutral particle in the nucleus; initially he did not consider these two neutral particles as distinct from each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">[15]</a></sup> The word "neutrino" entered the scientific vocabulary through <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Fermi" title="Enrico Fermi">Enrico Fermi</a>, who used it during a conference in Paris in July&#160;1932 and at the Solvay Conference in October&#160;1933, where Pauli also employed it. The name (the <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> equivalent of "little neutral one") was jokingly coined by <a href="/wiki/Edoardo_Amaldi" title="Edoardo Amaldi">Edoardo Amaldi</a> during a conversation with Fermi at the Institute of Physics of via Panisperna in Rome, in order to distinguish this light neutral particle from Chadwick\'s heavy neutron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Fermi%27s_interaction" title="Fermi\'s interaction">Fermi\'s theory of beta decay</a>, Chadwick\'s large neutral particle could decay to a proton, electron, and the smaller neutral particle (now called an <i>electron antineutrino</i>):\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">0</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> &#8594; <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>p<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#8722;</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">&#957;</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span></dd></dl>\n<p>Fermi\'s paper, written in 1934, unified Pauli\'s neutrino with <a href="/wiki/Paul_Dirac" title="Paul Dirac">Paul Dirac</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Werner_Heisenberg" title="Werner Heisenberg">Werner Heisenberg</a>\'s neutron&#8211;proton model and gave a solid theoretical basis for future experimental work. The journal <a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)"><i>Nature</i></a> rejected Fermi\'s paper, saying that the theory was "too remote from reality". He submitted the paper to an Italian journal, which accepted it, but the general lack of interest in his theory at that early date caused him to switch to experimental physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Close2012_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Close2012-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>24</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 1934 there was experimental evidence against Bohr\'s idea that energy conservation is invalid for beta decay: At the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solvay_conference" title="Solvay conference">Solvay conference</a> of that year, measurements of the energy spectra of beta particles (electrons) were reported, showing that there is a strict limit on the energy of electrons from each type of beta decay. Such a limit is not expected if the conservation of energy is invalid, in which case any amount of energy would be statistically available in at least a few decays. The natural explanation of the beta decay spectrum as first measured in 1934 was that only a limited (and conserved) amount of energy was available, and a new particle was sometimes taking a varying fraction of this limited energy, leaving the rest for the beta particle. Pauli made use of the occasion to publicly emphasize that the still-undetected "neutrino" must be an actual particle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Close2012_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Close2012-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>25</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_detection">Direct detection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Direct detection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clyde_Cowan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="120" data-file-width="120" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/53/Clyde_Cowan.jpg" width="120"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clyde_Cowan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Clyde Cowan conducting the neutrino experiment <i>circa</i> 1956</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1942, <a href="/wiki/Wang_Ganchang" title="Wang Ganchang">Wang Ganchang</a> first proposed the use of <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">beta capture</a> to experimentally detect neutrinos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> In the 20 July 1956 issue of <a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)"><i>Science</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/Clyde_Cowan" title="Clyde Cowan">Clyde Cowan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Reines" title="Frederick Reines">Frederick Reines</a>, F. B. Harrison, H. W. Kruse, and A. D. McGuire published confirmation that they had detected the neutrino,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup> a result that was rewarded almost forty years later with the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">1995 Nobel Prize</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noblp_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-noblp-24">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In this experiment, now known as the <a href="/wiki/Cowan%E2%80%93Reines_neutrino_experiment" title="Cowan&#8211;Reines neutrino experiment">Cowan&#8211;Reines neutrino experiment</a>, antineutrinos created in a nuclear reactor by beta decay reacted with protons to produce <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positrons</a>:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">&#957;</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>p<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> &#8594; <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">0</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></dd></dl>\n<p>The positron quickly finds an electron, and they <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">annihilate</a> each other. The two resulting <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a> (&#947;) are detectable. The neutron can be detected by its capture on an appropriate nucleus, releasing a gamma ray. The coincidence of both events &#8211; positron annihilation and neutron capture &#8211; gives a unique signature of an antineutrino interaction.\n</p><p>In February 1965, the first neutrino found in nature was identified in one of South Africa\'s gold mines by a group which included <a href="/wiki/Friedel_Sellschop" title="Friedel Sellschop">Friedel Sellschop</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> The experiment was performed in a specially prepared chamber at a depth of 3&#160;km in the ERPM mine near Boksburg. A plaque in the main building commemorates the discovery. The experiments also implemented a primitive neutrino astronomy and looked at issues of neutrino physics and weak interactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neutrino_flavor">Neutrino flavor<span id="Neutrino_flavors_anchor"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Neutrino flavor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The antineutrino discovered by Cowan and Reines is the antiparticle of the <a href="/wiki/Electron_neutrino" title="Electron neutrino">electron neutrino</a>.\n</p><p>In 1962, <a href="/wiki/Leon_M._Lederman" title="Leon M. Lederman">Leon M. Lederman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melvin_Schwartz" title="Melvin Schwartz">Melvin Schwartz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jack_Steinberger" title="Jack Steinberger">Jack Steinberger</a> showed that more than one type of neutrino exists by first detecting interactions of the <a href="/wiki/Muon" title="Muon">muon</a> neutrino (already hypothesised with the name <i>neutretto</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> which earned them the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">1988 Nobel Prize in Physics</a>.\n</p><p>When the third type of <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">lepton</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tau_(particle)" title="Tau (particle)">tau</a>, was discovered in 1975 at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stanford_Linear_Accelerator_Center" title="Stanford Linear Accelerator Center">Stanford Linear Accelerator Center</a>, it too was expected to have an associated neutrino (the tau neutrino). First evidence for this third neutrino type came from the observation of missing energy and momentum in tau decays analogous to the beta decay leading to the discovery of the electron neutrino. The first detection of tau neutrino interactions was announced in 2000 by the <a href="/wiki/DONUT" title="DONUT">DONUT collaboration</a> at <a href="/wiki/Fermilab" title="Fermilab">Fermilab</a>; its existence had already been inferred by both theoretical consistency and experimental data from the <a href="/wiki/Large_Electron%E2%80%93Positron_Collider" title="Large Electron&#8211;Positron Collider">Large Electron&#8211;Positron Collider</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tauonpress_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-tauonpress-28">[26]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_neutrino_problem">Solar neutrino problem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Solar neutrino problem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Solar_neutrino_problem" title="Solar neutrino problem">Solar neutrino problem</a></div>\n<p>In the 1960s, the now-famous <a href="/wiki/Homestake_experiment" title="Homestake experiment">Homestake experiment</a> made the first measurement of the flux of electron neutrinos arriving from the core of the Sun and found a value that was between one third and one half the number predicted by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Solar_Model" title="Standard Solar Model">Standard Solar Model</a>. This discrepancy, which became known as the <a href="/wiki/Solar_neutrino_problem" title="Solar neutrino problem">solar neutrino problem</a>, remained unresolved for some thirty years, while possible problems with both the experiment and the solar model were investigated, but none could be found. Eventually it was realized that both were actually correct, and that the discrepancy between them was due to neutrinos being more complex than was previously assumed. It was postulated that the three neutrinos had nonzero and slightly but indistinguishably different masses, and could therefore oscillate into undetectable flavors on their flight to the Earth.  This hypothesis was investigated by a new series of experiments, thereby opening a new major field of research that still continues.  Eventual confirmation of the phenomenon of neutrino oscillation led to two Nobel prizes, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raymond_Davis,_Jr." title="Raymond Davis, Jr.">Raymond Davis, Jr.</a>, who conceived and led the Homestake experiment, and to <a href="/wiki/Arthur_B._McDonald" title="Arthur B. McDonald">Art McDonald</a>, who led the <a href="/wiki/Sudbury_Neutrino_Observatory" title="Sudbury Neutrino Observatory">SNO</a> experiment, which could detect all of the neutrino flavors and found no deficit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SNO2001_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-SNO2001-29">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oscillation">Oscillation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Oscillation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neutrino_oscillation" title="Neutrino oscillation">Neutrino oscillation</a></div>\n<p>A practical method for investigating neutrino oscillations was first suggested by <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Pontecorvo" title="Bruno Pontecorvo">Bruno Pontecorvo</a> in 1957 using an analogy with <a href="/wiki/Kaon" title="Kaon">kaon</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaon_oscillation" title="Kaon oscillation">oscillations</a>; over the subsequent 10 years he developed the mathematical formalism and the modern formulation of vacuum oscillations. In 1985 <a href="/wiki/Stanislav_Mikheyev" title="Stanislav Mikheyev">Stanislav Mikheyev</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexei_Smirnov_(physicist)" title="Alexei Smirnov (physicist)">Alexei Smirnov</a> (expanding on 1978 work by <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Wolfenstein" title="Lincoln Wolfenstein">Lincoln Wolfenstein</a>) noted that flavor oscillations can be modified when neutrinos propagate through matter. This so-called <a href="/wiki/Mikheyev%E2%80%93Smirnov%E2%80%93Wolfenstein_effect" title="Mikheyev&#8211;Smirnov&#8211;Wolfenstein effect">Mikheyev&#8211;Smirnov&#8211;Wolfenstein effect</a> (MSW effect) is important to understand because many neutrinos emitted by fusion in the Sun pass through the dense matter in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_core" title="Solar core">solar core</a> (where essentially all solar fusion takes place) on their way to detectors on Earth.\n</p><p>Starting in 1998, experiments began to show that solar and atmospheric neutrinos change flavors (see <a href="/wiki/Super-Kamiokande" title="Super-Kamiokande">Super-Kamiokande</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sudbury_Neutrino_Observatory" title="Sudbury Neutrino Observatory">Sudbury Neutrino Observatory</a>). This resolved the solar neutrino problem: the electron neutrinos produced in the Sun had partly changed into other flavors which the experiments could not detect.\n</p><p>Although individual experiments, such as the set of solar neutrino experiments, are consistent with non-oscillatory mechanisms of neutrino flavor conversion, taken altogether, neutrino experiments imply the existence of neutrino oscillations. Especially relevant in this context are the reactor experiment <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KamLAND" title="KamLAND">KamLAND</a> and the accelerator experiments such as <a href="/wiki/MINOS" title="MINOS">MINOS</a>. The KamLAND experiment has indeed identified oscillations as the neutrino flavor conversion mechanism involved in the solar electron neutrinos. Similarly MINOS confirms the oscillation of atmospheric neutrinos and gives a better determination of the mass squared splitting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Takaaki_Kajita" title="Takaaki Kajita">Takaaki Kajita</a> of Japan and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_B._McDonald" title="Arthur B. McDonald">Arthur B. McDonald</a> of Canada received the 2015 Nobel Prize for Physics for their landmark finding, theoretical and experimental, that neutrinos can change flavors.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmic_neutrinos">Cosmic neutrinos</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cosmic neutrinos">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Raymond Davis, Jr. and <a href="/wiki/Masatoshi_Koshiba" title="Masatoshi Koshiba">Masatoshi Koshiba</a> were jointly awarded the 2002 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a>. Both conducted pioneering work on <a href="/wiki/Solar_neutrino" title="Solar neutrino">solar neutrino</a> detection, and Koshiba\'s work also resulted in the first real-time observation of neutrinos from the <a href="/wiki/SN_1987A" title="SN 1987A">SN 1987A</a> <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> in the nearby <a href="/wiki/Large_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Large Magellanic Cloud">Large Magellanic Cloud</a>. These efforts marked the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Neutrino_astronomy" title="Neutrino astronomy">neutrino astronomy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pagliaroli2009_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pagliaroli2009-31">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/SN_1987A" title="SN 1987A">SN 1987A</a> represents the only verified detection of neutrinos from a supernova. However, many stars have gone supernova in our galaxy, leaving a theorized <a href="/wiki/Diffuse_supernova_neutrino_background" title="Diffuse supernova neutrino background">diffuse supernova neutrino background</a>. The same concept extends to the entire universe, giving the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_neutrino_background" title="Cosmic neutrino background">cosmic neutrino background</a>, or relic neutrinos.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties_and_reactions">Properties and reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Properties and reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The neutrino has half-integer <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a> (&#189;&#8239;&#295;) and is therefore a <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermion</a>. Also being leptons, neutrinos have been observed to interact through only the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_nuclear_force" title="Weak nuclear force">weak force</a>, although it is assumed that they also interact gravitationally.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Flavor.2C_mass.2C_and_their_mixing"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Flavor,_mass,_and_their_mixing">Flavor, mass, and their mixing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Flavor, mass, and their mixing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Weak interactions create neutrinos in one of three leptonic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flavor_(particle_physics)" title="Flavor (particle physics)">flavors</a>: <a href="/wiki/Electron_neutrino" title="Electron neutrino">electron neutrinos</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#957;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Muon_neutrino" title="Muon neutrino">muon neutrinos</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#957;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#956;</sub></span></span>), or <a href="/wiki/Tau_neutrino" title="Tau neutrino">tau neutrinos</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#957;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#964;</sub></span></span>), in association with the corresponding <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muon" title="Muon">muon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tau_(particle)" title="Tau (particle)">tau</a> charged leptons, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PDG_mixing_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-PDG_mixing-32">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although neutrinos were long believed to be massless, it is now known that there are also three discrete neutrino masses, but they do not correspond uniquely to the three flavors. Although only differences of squares of the three mass values are known as of 2016,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Capozzi_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Capozzi-4">[4]</a></sup> experiments have shown that these masses are tiny in magnitude. From <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmological</a> measurements, it has been calculated that the sum of the three neutrino masses must be less than one millionth that of the electron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mertens_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mertens-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PDG_mass_sum_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-PDG_mass_sum-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>More formally, neutrino flavor <a href="/wiki/Eigenvalues_and_eigenvectors" title="Eigenvalues and eigenvectors">eigenstates</a> are not the same as the neutrino mass eigenstates (simply labelled 1, 2, 3). As of 2016, it is not known which of these three is the heaviest. In analogy with the mass hierarchy of the charged leptons, the configuration with mass&#160;2 being lighter than mass&#160;3 is conventionally called the "normal hierarchy", while in the "inverted hierarchy", the opposite would hold. Several major experimental efforts are underway to help establish which is correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hierarchy_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-hierarchy-33">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A neutrino created in a specific flavor eigenstate is in an associated specific <a href="/wiki/Quantum_superposition" title="Quantum superposition">quantum superposition</a> of all three mass eigenstates.  This is possible because the three masses differ so little that they cannot be experimentally distinguished within any practical flight path, due to the <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty_principle" title="Uncertainty principle">uncertainty principle</a>.  The proportion of each mass state in the produced pure flavor state has been found to depend strongly on that flavor. The relationship between flavor and mass eigenstates is encoded in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMNS_matrix" title="PMNS matrix">PMNS matrix</a>. Experiments have established values for the elements of this matrix.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Capozzi_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Capozzi-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The existence of a neutrino mass allows the possibility of a tiny neutrino <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_moment" title="Magnetic moment">magnetic moment</a>, in which case neutrinos could interact electromagnetically as well; no such interaction has been discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flavor_oscillations">Flavor oscillations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Flavor oscillations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neutrino_oscillation" title="Neutrino oscillation">Neutrino oscillation</a></div>\n<p>Neutrinos <a href="/wiki/Neutrino_oscillation" title="Neutrino oscillation">oscillate</a> between different flavors in flight. For example, an electron neutrino produced in a <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> reaction may interact in a distant detector as a muon or tau neutrino, as defined by the flavor of the charged lepton produced in the detector. This oscillation occurs because the three mass state components of the produced flavor travel at slightly different speeds, so that their quantum mechanical <a href="/wiki/Wave_packet" title="Wave packet">wave packets</a> develop relative <a href="/wiki/Phase_(waves)#phase_shift" title="Phase (waves)">phase shifts</a> that change how they combine to produce a varying superposition of three flavors. Each flavor component thereby oscillates <a href="/wiki/Sine_wave" title="Sine wave">sinusoidally</a> as the neutrino travels, with the flavors varying in relative strengths.  The relative flavor proportions when the neutrino interacts represent the relative probabilities for that flavor of interaction to produce the corresponding flavor of charged lepton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GrossmanLipkin_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-GrossmanLipkin-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bilenky_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bilenky-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are other possibilities in which neutrino could oscillate even if they were massless. If <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_covariance" title="Lorentz covariance">Lorentz symmetry</a> were not an exact symmetry, neutrinos could experience <a href="/wiki/Lorentz-violating_neutrino_oscillations" title="Lorentz-violating neutrino oscillations">Lorentz-violating oscillations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Mikheyev.E2.80.93Smirnov.E2.80.93Wolfenstein_effect"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mikheyev&#8211;Smirnov&#8211;Wolfenstein_effect">Mikheyev&#8211;Smirnov&#8211;Wolfenstein effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Mikheyev&#8211;Smirnov&#8211;Wolfenstein effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mikheyev%E2%80%93Smirnov%E2%80%93Wolfenstein_effect" title="Mikheyev&#8211;Smirnov&#8211;Wolfenstein effect">Mikheyev&#8211;Smirnov&#8211;Wolfenstein effect</a></div>\n<p>Neutrinos traveling through matter, in general, undergo a process analogous to <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light#In_a_medium" title="Speed of light">light traveling through a transparent material</a>. This process is not directly observable because it does not produce <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a>, but gives rise to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MSW_effect" title="MSW effect">MSW effect</a>. Only a small fraction of the neutrino\'s energy is transferred to the material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antineutrinos">Antineutrinos</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Antineutrinos">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em"><a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">Antimatter</a></th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0.2em 0 0.4em"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PositronDiscovery.jpg"><img alt="PositronDiscovery.jpg" data-file-height="2374" data-file-width="2408" decoding="async" height="69" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/PositronDiscovery.jpg/70px-PositronDiscovery.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/PositronDiscovery.jpg/105px-PositronDiscovery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/PositronDiscovery.jpg/140px-PositronDiscovery.jpg 2x" width="70"/></a></td></tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;text-align:left; padding:2px; padding-right:4px; border-width:0 1px">\n<div style="width:100%; background:lavender; padding-left:2px;"><a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">Annihilation</a></div></td></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding-bottom:2px">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;padding-left:2px; background:lavender">Devices</div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">Particle accelerator</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Penning_trap" title="Penning trap">Penning trap</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Cloud_chamber" title="Cloud chamber">Wilson chamber</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding-bottom:2px">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;padding-left:2px; background:lavender"><a href="/wiki/Antiparticle" title="Antiparticle">Antiparticles</a></div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">Positron</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Antiproton" title="Antiproton">Antiproton</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Antineutron" title="Antineutron">Antineutron</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding-bottom:2px">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;padding-left:2px; background:lavender">Uses</div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Positron_emission_tomography" title="Positron emission tomography">Positron emission tomography</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Antimatter#Fuel" title="Antimatter">Fuel</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding-bottom:2px">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;padding-left:2px; background:lavender">Bodies</div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ALPHA_Collaboration" title="ALPHA Collaboration">ALPHA Collaboration</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ATHENA" title="ATHENA">ATHENA</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ATRAP" title="ATRAP">ATRAP</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/CERN" title="CERN">CERN</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Relativistic_Heavy_Ion_Collider" title="Relativistic Heavy Ion Collider">RHIC</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding-bottom:2px">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;padding-left:2px; background:lavender">People</div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Paul_Dirac" title="Paul Dirac">Paul Dirac</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Carl_David_Anderson" title="Carl David Anderson">Carl David Anderson</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Andrei_Sakharov" title="Andrei Sakharov">Andrei Sakharov</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;padding:0 2px"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Antimatter" title="Template:Antimatter"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Antimatter" title="Template talk:Antimatter"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Antimatter&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>For each neutrino, there also exists a corresponding <a href="/wiki/Antiparticle" title="Antiparticle">antiparticle</a>, called an <i>antineutrino</i>, which also has no electric charge and half-integer spin. They are distinguished from the neutrinos by having opposite signs of <a href="/wiki/Lepton_number" title="Lepton number">lepton number</a> and opposite <a href="/wiki/Chirality_(physics)" title="Chirality (physics)">chirality</a>. As of 2016, no evidence has been found for any other difference.  In all observations so far of leptonic processes (despite extensive and continuing searches for exceptions), there is no overall change in lepton number; for example, if total lepton number is zero in the initial state, electron neutrinos appear in the final state together with only positrons (anti-electrons) or electron-antineutrinos, and electron antineutrinos with electrons or electron neutrinos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neutrinos_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Neutrinos-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conservation_of_lepton_number_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Conservation_of_lepton_number-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Antineutrinos are produced in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear</a> <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> together with a <a href="/wiki/Beta_particle" title="Beta particle">beta particle</a>, in which, e.g., a neutron decays into a proton, electron, and antineutrino. All antineutrinos observed thus far possess right-handed <a href="/wiki/Helicity_(particle_physics)" title="Helicity (particle physics)">helicity</a> (i.e. only one of the two possible spin states has ever been seen), while neutrinos are left-handed. Nevertheless, as neutrinos have mass, their helicity is <a href="/wiki/Frame_of_reference" title="Frame of reference">frame</a>-dependent, so it is the related frame-independent property of chirality that is relevant here.\n</p><p>Antineutrinos were first detected as a result of their interaction with protons in a large tank of water. This was installed next to a nuclear reactor as a controllable source of the antineutrinos (See: <a href="/wiki/Cowan%E2%80%93Reines_neutrino_experiment" title="Cowan&#8211;Reines neutrino experiment">Cowan&#8211;Reines neutrino experiment</a>).\nResearchers around the world have begun to investigate the possibility of using antineutrinos for reactor monitoring in the context of preventing the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">proliferation of nuclear weapons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Majorana_mass">Majorana mass</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Majorana mass">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Seesaw_mechanism" title="Seesaw mechanism">seesaw mechanism</a></div>\n<p>Because antineutrinos and neutrinos are neutral particles, it is possible that they are the same particle. Particles that have this property are known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majorana_particle" title="Majorana particle">Majorana particles</a>, after the Italian physicist <a href="/wiki/Ettore_Majorana" title="Ettore Majorana">Ettore Majorana</a> who first proposed the concept. For the case of neutrinos this theory has gained popularity as it can be used, in combination with the <a href="/wiki/Seesaw_mechanism" title="Seesaw mechanism">seesaw mechanism</a>, to explain why neutrino masses are so small compared to those of the other elementary particles, such as electrons or quarks. Majorana neutrinos have the property that the neutrino and antineutrino could be distinguished only by <a href="/wiki/Chirality_(physics)" title="Chirality (physics)">chirality</a>; what experiments observe as a difference between the neutrino and antineutrino could simply be due to one particle with two possible chiralities.\n</p><p>It is not yet known whether neutrinos are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majorana_particle" title="Majorana particle">Majorana</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dirac_fermion" title="Dirac fermion">Dirac</a> particles; it is possible to test this property experimentally. For example, if neutrinos are indeed Majorana particles, then lepton-number violating processes such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutrinoless_double_beta_decay" title="Neutrinoless double beta decay">neutrinoless double beta decay</a> would be allowed, while they would not if neutrinos are <a href="/wiki/Dirac_fermion" title="Dirac fermion">Dirac</a> particles. Several experiments have been and are being conducted to search for this process, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Germanium_Detector_Array" title="Germanium Detector Array">GERDA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neutrino-fundamentals_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-neutrino-fundamentals-40">[38]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/SNO%2B" title="SNO+">SNO+</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_neutrino_background" title="Cosmic neutrino background">cosmic neutrino background</a> is also a probe of whether neutrinos are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majorana_particles" title="Majorana particles">Majorana particles</a>, since there should be a different number of cosmic neutrinos detected in either the Dirac or Majorana case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_reactions">Nuclear reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Nuclear reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Neutrinos can interact with a nucleus, changing it to another nucleus. This process is used in radiochemical <a href="/wiki/Neutrino_detector" title="Neutrino detector">neutrino detectors</a>. In this case, the energy levels and spin states within the target nucleus have to be taken into account to estimate the probability for an interaction. In general the interaction probability increases with the number of neutrons and protons within a nucleus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SNO2001_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-SNO2001-29">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhLB._2005_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-PhLB._2005-43">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is very hard to uniquely identify neutrino interactions among the natural background of radioactivity. For this reason, in early experiments a special reaction channel was chosen to facilitate the identification: the interaction of an antineutrino with one of the hydrogen nuclei in the water molecules. A hydrogen nucleus is a single proton, so simultaneous nuclear interactions, which would occur within a heavier nucleus, don\'t need to be considered for the detection experiment. Within a cubic metre of water placed right outside a nuclear reactor, only relatively few such interactions can be recorded, but the setup is now used for measuring the reactor\'s plutonium production rate.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Induced_fission">Induced fission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Induced fission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Very much like <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> do in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>, neutrinos can induce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_reaction" title="Fission reaction">fission reactions</a> within heavy <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nuclei</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup> So far, this reaction has not been measured in a laboratory, but is predicted to happen within stars and supernovae. The process affects the <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements#Abundance_of_elements_in_the_Universe" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">abundance of isotopes</a> seen in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhLB._2005_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-PhLB._2005-43">[41]</a></sup> Neutrino fission of <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> nuclei has been observed in the <a href="/wiki/Sudbury_Neutrino_Observatory" title="Sudbury Neutrino Observatory">Sudbury Neutrino Observatory</a>, which uses a <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">heavy water</a> detector.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="No_self_interaction">No self interaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: No self interaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Observations of the cosmic microwave background suggest that neutrinos do not interact with themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable floatright">\n<caption>Neutrinos in the Standard Model of elementary particles\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Fermion\n</th>\n<th>Symbol\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#956;</sub></span></span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="background:#efefef;">Muon antineutrino\n</td>\n<td><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#956;</sub></span></span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="3" style="background:#ffdead;">Generation 3\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="background:#efefef;">Tau neutrino\n</td>\n<td><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#957;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#964;</sub></span></span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="background:#efefef;">Tau antineutrino\n</td>\n<td><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">&#957;</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#964;</sub></span></span>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>There are three known types (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flavor_(particle_physics)" title="Flavor (particle physics)">flavors</a></i>) of neutrinos: electron neutrino <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#957;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span>, muon neutrino <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#957;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#956;</sub></span></span> and tau neutrino <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#957;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#964;</sub></span></span>, named after their partner <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">leptons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> (see table at right). The current best measurement of the number of neutrino types comes from observing the decay of the <a href="/wiki/W_and_Z_bosons" title="W and Z bosons">Z boson</a>. This particle can decay into any light neutrino and its antineutrino, and the more types of light neutrinos<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[c]</a></sup> available, the shorter the lifetime of the Z&#160;boson. Measurements of the Z lifetime have shown that the number of light neutrino flavors that couple to the Z is 3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PDG_mixing_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-PDG_mixing-32">[30]</a></sup> The correspondence between the six <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a> in the Standard Model and the six leptons, among them the three neutrinos, suggests to physicists\' intuition that there should be exactly three types of neutrino. Proof that there are only three kinds of neutrinos remains an elusive goal of particle physics.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>There are several active research areas involving the neutrino. Some are concerned with testing predictions of neutrino behavior. Other research is focused on measurement of unknown properties of neutrinos, especially their masses and <a href="/wiki/CP_violation" title="CP violation">CP violation</a>, as they cannot be predicted with existing theories.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Detectors_near_artificial_neutrino_sources">Detectors near artificial neutrino sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Detectors near artificial neutrino sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>International scientific collaborations install large neutrino detectors near nuclear reactors or in neutrino beams from particle accelerators to better constrain the neutrino masses and the values for the magnitude and rates of oscillations between neutrino flavors. These experiments are thereby searching for the existence of CP violation in the neutrino sector; that is, whether or not the laws of physics treat neutrinos and antineutrinos differently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Capozzi_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Capozzi-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/KATRIN" title="KATRIN">KATRIN</a> experiment in Germany has begun to acquire data in June 2018<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-katrin_start_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-katrin_start-47">[44]</a></sup> to determine the value of the mass of the electron neutrino, with other approaches to this problem in the planning stages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mertens_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mertens-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tests_of_neutrino_oscillation">Tests of neutrino oscillation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Tests of neutrino oscillation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On 19&#160;July 2013, the results from the <a href="/wiki/T2K_experiment" title="T2K experiment">T2K experiment</a> presented at the <a href="/wiki/European_Physical_Society" title="European Physical Society">European Physical Society</a> Conference on High Energy Physics in Stockholm, Sweden, confirmed neutrino oscillation theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhysicsNews20130719_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-PhysicsNews20130719-48">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC20130719_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC20130719-49">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravitational_effects">Gravitational effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Gravitational effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Despite their tiny masses, neutrinos are so numerous that their gravitational force can influence other matter in the universe.\n</p><p>The three known neutrino flavors are the only established <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particle</a> candidates for <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>, specifically <a href="/wiki/Hot_dark_matter" title="Hot dark matter">hot dark matter</a>, although that possibility appears to be largely ruled out by observations of the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a>. If heavier sterile neutrinos exist, they might serve as <a href="/wiki/Warm_dark_matter" title="Warm dark matter">warm dark matter</a>, which still seems plausible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sterile_neutrino_searches">Sterile neutrino searches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Sterile neutrino searches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Other efforts search for evidence of a <a href="/wiki/Sterile_neutrino" title="Sterile neutrino">sterile neutrino</a> &#8211; a fourth neutrino flavor that does not interact with matter like the three known neutrino flavors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quanta_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quanta-51">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WhitePaper_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-WhitePaper-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lasserre_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lasserre-53">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giunti_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Giunti-54">[51]</a></sup> The possibility of <a href="/wiki/Sterile_neutrino" title="Sterile neutrino"><i>sterile</i> neutrinos</a> is unaffected by the Z-boson decay measurements described above: If their mass is greater than half the Z-boson\'s mass, they would not be a decay product. Therefore, heavy sterile neutrinos would have a mass of at least 45.6&#160;GeV.\n</p><p>The existence of such particles is in fact hinted by experimental data from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LSND" title="LSND">LSND</a> experiment. On the other hand, the currently running <a href="/wiki/MiniBooNE" title="MiniBooNE">MiniBooNE</a> experiment suggested that sterile neutrinos are not required to explain the experimental data,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhysRevD_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-PhysRevD-55">[52]</a></sup> although the latest research into this area is on-going and anomalies in the MiniBooNE data may allow for exotic neutrino types, including sterile neutrinos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup> A recent re-analysis of reference electron spectra data from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Institut_Laue-Langevin" title="Institut Laue-Langevin">Institut Laue-Langevin</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup> has also hinted at a fourth, sterile neutrino.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to an analysis published in 2010, data from the <a href="/wiki/Wilkinson_Microwave_Anisotropy_Probe" title="Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe">Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">cosmic background radiation</a> is compatible with either three or four types of neutrinos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[56]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neutrinoless_double-beta_decay_searches">Neutrinoless double-beta decay searches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Neutrinoless double-beta decay searches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Another hypothesis concerns "neutrinoless double-beta decay", which, if it exists, would violate lepton number conservation and imply a minuscule splitting (or difference) between the physical masses of what are conventionally called a "neutrino" and its corresponding "antineutrino" having the opposite sign in its lepton number. Searches for this mechanism are underway but have not yet found strong evidence for it. If they were to, then what are now called antineutrinos could not be true antiparticles. The resulting <i>six</i> distinct neutrinos would have no distinct antiparticle partner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PDG_0nu2beta_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-PDG_0nu2beta-60">[57]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">Cosmic ray</a> neutrino experiments detect neutrinos from space to study both the nature of neutrinos and the cosmic sources producing them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IceCube_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-IceCube-61">[58]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Speed">Speed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Speed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Measurements_of_neutrino_speed" title="Measurements of neutrino speed">Measurements of neutrino speed</a></div>\n<p>Before neutrinos were found to oscillate, they were generally assumed to be massless, propagating at the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>. According to the theory of <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a>, the question of neutrino <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a> is closely related to their <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>: if neutrinos are massless, they must travel at the speed of light, and if they have mass they cannot reach the speed of light. Due to their tiny mass, the predicted speed is extremely close to the speed of light in all experiments, and current detectors are not sensitive to the expected difference.\n</p><p>Also some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorentz_violation" title="Lorentz violation">Lorentz-violating</a> variants of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_gravity" title="Quantum gravity">quantum gravity</a> might allow faster-than-light neutrinos. A comprehensive framework for Lorentz violations is the <a href="/wiki/Standard-Model_Extension" title="Standard-Model Extension">Standard-Model Extension</a> (SME).\n</p><p>In the early 1980s, first measurements of neutrino speed were done using pulsed <a href="/wiki/Pion" title="Pion">pion</a> beams (produced by pulsed proton beams hitting a target). The pions decayed producing neutrinos, and the neutrino interactions observed within a time window in a detector at a distance were consistent with the speed of light.
