This measurement was repeated in 2007 using the <a href="/wiki/MINOS" title="MINOS">MINOS</a> detectors, which found the speed of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6990480652946099999&#9824;</span>3&#160;<a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">GeV</a></span> neutrinos to be, at the 99% confidence level, in the range between <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999999976000000000&#9824;</span>0.999<span style="margin-left:.25em;">976</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light"><i>c</i></a></span> and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000100012600000000&#9824;</span>1.000<span style="margin-left:.25em;">126</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light"><i>c</i></a></span>.  The central value of 1.000051<i>c</i> is higher than the speed of light but is also consistent with a velocity of exactly <i>c</i> or even slightly less. This measurement set an upper bound on the mass of the muon neutrino of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6988801088243500000&#9824;</span>50&#160;MeV</span> at 99% <a href="/wiki/Confidence_interval" title="Confidence interval">confidence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup> After the detectors for the project were upgraded in 2012, MINOS refined their initial result and found agreement with the speed of light, with the difference in the arrival time of neutrinos and light of &#8722;0.0006% (&#177;0.0012%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A similar observation was made, on a much larger scale, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supernova_1987A" title="Supernova 1987A">supernova 1987A</a> (SN 1987A). 10&#160;MeV antineutrinos from the supernova were detected within a time window that was consistent with the speed of light for the neutrinos. So far, all measurements of neutrino speed have been consistent with the speed of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September&#160;2011, the <a href="/wiki/OPERA_experiment" title="OPERA experiment">OPERA collaboration</a> released calculations showing velocities of 17&#160;GeV and 28&#160;GeV neutrinos exceeding the speed of light in their experiments (see <a href="/wiki/Faster-than-light_neutrino_anomaly" title="Faster-than-light neutrino anomaly">Faster-than-light neutrino anomaly</a>). In November&#160;2011, OPERA repeated its experiment with changes so that the speed could be determined individually for each detected neutrino. The results showed the same faster-than-light speed. In February&#160;2012, reports came out that the results may have been caused by a loose fiber optic cable attached to one of the atomic clocks which measured the departure and arrival times of the neutrinos. An independent recreation of the experiment in the same laboratory by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ICARUS_(experiment)" title="ICARUS (experiment)">ICARUS</a> found no discernible difference between the speed of a neutrino and the speed of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arxiv-20120316_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arxiv-20120316-67">[64]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In June&#160;2012, CERN announced that new measurements conducted by all four Gran Sasso experiments (OPERA, ICARUS, <a href="/wiki/Borexino" title="Borexino">Borexino</a> and <a href="/wiki/Large_Volume_Detector" title="Large Volume Detector">LVD</a>) found agreement between the speed of light and the speed of neutrinos, finally refuting the initial OPERA claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[65]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mass">Mass</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Mass">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table style="float:right; width:30%; margin:0em 1em 1em 1em; background:#eee; border:#ccc solid">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_dropshade.png"><img alt="Question dropshade.png" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/20px-Question_dropshade.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/30px-Question_dropshade.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/40px-Question_dropshade.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>\n<td><b>Unsolved problem in physics</b>:<br/><div style="padding:5px; font-style:italic;">Can we measure the neutrino masses? Do neutrinos follow <a href="/wiki/Fermi%E2%80%93Dirac_statistics" title="Fermi&#8211;Dirac statistics">Dirac</a> or <a href="/wiki/Majorana_fermion" title="Majorana fermion">Majorana</a> statistics?</div><small><a href="/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="List of unsolved problems in physics">(more unsolved problems in physics)</a></small>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> of particle physics assumed that neutrinos are massless. The experimentally established phenomenon of neutrino oscillation, which mixes neutrino flavour states with neutrino mass states (analogously to <a href="/wiki/Cabibbo%E2%80%93Kobayashi%E2%80%93Maskawa_matrix" title="Cabibbo&#8211;Kobayashi&#8211;Maskawa matrix">CKM mixing</a>), requires neutrinos to have nonzero masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhysRevD1_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-PhysRevD1-69">[66]</a></sup> Massive neutrinos were originally conceived by <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Pontecorvo" title="Bruno Pontecorvo">Bruno Pontecorvo</a> in the 1950s. Enhancing the basic framework to accommodate their mass is straightforward by adding a right-handed Lagrangian.\n</p><p>Providing for neutrino mass can be done in two ways, and some proposals use both:\n</p>\n<ol><li>If, like other fundamental Standard Model particles, mass is generated by the <a href="/wiki/Dirac_fermion" title="Dirac fermion">Dirac mechanism</a>, then the framework would require an <a href="/wiki/Special_unitary_group#n_=_2" title="Special unitary group">SU(2) singlet</a>. This particle would have the <a href="/wiki/Yukawa_interaction" title="Yukawa interaction">Yukawa interactions</a> with the neutral component of the <a href="/wiki/Higgs_boson" title="Higgs boson">Higgs</a> <a href="/wiki/Doublet_state" title="Doublet state">doublet</a>, but otherwise would have no interactions with Standard Model particles, so is called a "sterile" neutrino.</li>\n<li>Or, mass can be generated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majorana_mass" title="Majorana mass">Majorana mechanism</a>, which would require the neutrino and antineutrino to be the same particle.</li></ol>\n<p>The strongest upper limit on the masses of neutrinos comes from <a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology" title="Physical cosmology">cosmology</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> model predicts that there is a fixed ratio between the number of neutrinos and the number of <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">cosmic microwave background</a>. If the total energy of all three types of neutrinos exceeded an average of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6982801088243500000&#9824;</span>50&#160;<a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">eV</a></span> per neutrino, there would be so much mass in the universe that it would collapse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hut1979_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hut1979-70">[67]</a></sup> This limit can be circumvented by assuming that the neutrino is unstable, but there are limits within the Standard Model that make this difficult. A much more stringent constraint comes from a careful analysis of cosmological data, such as the cosmic microwave background radiation, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galaxy_survey" title="Galaxy survey">galaxy surveys</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Lyman-alpha_forest" title="Lyman-alpha forest">Lyman-alpha forest</a>. These indicate that the summed masses of the three neutrinos must be less than <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6980480652946099999&#9824;</span>0.3&#160;eV</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Nobel prize in Physics 2015 was awarded to both <a href="/wiki/Takaaki_Kajita" title="Takaaki Kajita">Takaaki Kajita</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_B._McDonald" title="Arthur B. McDonald">Arthur B. McDonald</a> for their experimental discovery of neutrino oscillations, which demonstrates that neutrinos have mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1998, research results at the <a href="/wiki/Super-Kamiokande" title="Super-Kamiokande">Super-Kamiokande</a> neutrino detector determined that neutrinos can oscillate from one flavor to another, which requires that they must have a nonzero mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup> While this shows that neutrinos have mass, the absolute neutrino mass scale is still not known. This is because neutrino oscillations are sensitive only to the difference in the squares of the masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup> The best estimate of the difference in the squares of the masses of mass eigenstates 1 and 2 was published by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KamLAND" title="KamLAND">KamLAND</a> in 2005: <span style="white-space:nowrap">|&#916;<i>m</i><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">21</sub></span>| = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6995790000000000000&#9824;</span>0.000<span style="margin-left:.25em;">079</span>&#160;eV<sup>2</sup></span>.</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup> In 2006, the <a href="/wiki/MINOS" title="MINOS">MINOS</a> experiment measured oscillations from an intense muon neutrino beam, determining the difference in the squares of the masses between neutrino mass eigenstates 2 and 3. The initial results indicate <span style="white-space:nowrap">|&#916;<i>m</i><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">32</sub></span>| = 0.0027 eV<sup>2</sup></span>, consistent with previous results from Super-Kamiokande.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup> Since |&#916;<i>m</i><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">32</sub></span>| is the difference of two squared masses, at least one of them has to have a value which is at least the square root of this value. Thus, there exists at least one neutrino mass eigenstate with a mass of at least <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6979801088243500000&#9824;</span>0.05&#160;eV</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2009, lensing data of a galaxy cluster were analyzed to predict a neutrino mass of about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6981240326473050000&#9824;</span>1.5&#160;eV</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup> This surprisingly high value requires that the three neutrino masses be nearly equal, with neutrino oscillations on the order of <i><a href="/wiki/Milli-" title="Milli-">milli</a></i> electron-Volts. In 2016 this was updated to a mass of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6981296402650095000&#9824;</span>1.85&#160;eV</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup> It predicts 3 sterile neutrinos of the same mass, stems with the Planck dark matter fraction and the non-observation of neutrinoless double beta decay. The masses lie below the Mainz-Troitsk upper bound of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6981352478827140000&#9824;</span>2.2&#160;eV</span> for the electron antineutrino.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup> The latter is being tested since June 2018 in the <a href="/wiki/KATRIN" title="KATRIN">KATRIN</a> experiment, that searches for a mass between <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6980320435297400000&#9824;</span>0.2&#160;eV</span> and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6981320435297400000&#9824;</span>2&#160;eV</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-katrin_start_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-katrin_start-47">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A number of efforts are under way to directly determine the absolute neutrino mass scale in laboratory experiments. The methods applied involve nuclear beta decay (<a href="/wiki/KATRIN" title="KATRIN">KATRIN</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=MARE&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="MARE (page does not exist)">MARE</a>).\n</p><p>On 31 May 2010, <a href="/wiki/OPERA_experiment" title="OPERA experiment">OPERA</a> researchers observed the first <a href="/wiki/Tau_neutrino" title="Tau neutrino">tau neutrino</a> candidate event in a <a href="/wiki/Muon_neutrino" title="Muon neutrino">muon neutrino</a> beam, the first time this transformation in neutrinos had been observed, providing further evidence that they have mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In July&#160;2010, the 3-D MegaZ DR7 galaxy survey reported that they had measured a limit of the combined mass of the three neutrino varieties to be less than <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6980448609416360000&#9824;</span>0.28&#160;eV</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup> A tighter upper bound yet for this sum of masses,  <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6980368500592010000&#9824;</span>0.23&#160;eV</span>, was reported in March 2013 by the <a href="/wiki/Planck_(spacecraft)" title="Planck (spacecraft)">Planck collaboration</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup> whereas a February 2014 result estimates the sum as 0.320 &#177; 0.081&#160;eV based on discrepancies between the cosmological consequences implied by Planck\'s detailed measurements of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_Microwave_Background" title="Cosmic Microwave Background">Cosmic Microwave Background</a> and predictions arising from observing other phenomena, combined with the assumption that neutrinos are responsible for the observed weaker <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_lensing" title="Gravitational lensing">gravitational lensing</a> than would be expected from massless neutrinos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>If the neutrino is a <a href="/wiki/Majorana_fermion" title="Majorana fermion">Majorana particle</a>, the mass may be calculated by finding the <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of <a href="/wiki/Double_beta_decay" title="Double beta decay">neutrinoless double-beta decay</a> of certain nuclei. The current lowest upper limit on the Majorana mass of the neutrino has been set by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KamLAND" title="KamLAND">KamLAND</a>-Zen: 0.060&#8211;0.161&#160;eV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Size">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Standard Model neutrinos are fundamental point-like particles, without any width or volume. Since the neutrino is an elementary particle it does not have a size in the same sense as everyday objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup> An effective size can be defined using their electroweak <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(physics)" title="Cross section (physics)">cross section</a> (apparent size in <a href="/wiki/Electroweak_interaction" title="Electroweak interaction">electroweak interaction</a>). The characteristic areas for the electroweak interaction are measured in units called <a href="/wiki/Barn_(unit)" title="Barn (unit)">nanobarns</a>&#160;(nb) which are 10<sup>&#8722;33</sup>&#160;cm&#178; or 10<sup>&#8722;37</sup>&#160;m&#178;, roughly the area of a disc a little more than 0.3&#160;<a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(length)#1_attometre" title="Orders of magnitude (length)">attometer</a> in diameter, or about 1 billionth of the size of a uranium nucleus. The electron neutrino cross section is 3.2&#160; nanobarns the muon neutrino cross section is 1.7&#160;nanobarns, and the tau neutrino 1.0&#160;nanobarn. These scattering cross sections depend on no other properties than the masses of the corresponding charged leptons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup> This size is relevant only to the probability of scattering. Properties associated with conventional "size" are absent: neutrinos cannot be condensed to form a separate uniform substance and they have no minimal distance between them.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chirality">Chirality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Chirality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sterile_neutrino" title="Sterile neutrino">Sterile neutrino</a></div>\n<p>Experimental results show that (nearly) all produced and observed neutrinos have left-handed <a href="/wiki/Helicity_(particle_physics)" title="Helicity (particle physics)">helicities</a> (spins antiparallel to <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momenta</a>), and all antineutrinos have right-handed helicities, within the margin of error.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup> In the massless limit, it means that only one of two possible <a href="/wiki/Chirality_(physics)" title="Chirality (physics)">chiralities</a> is observed for either particle. These are the only chiralities included in the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> of particle interactions.\n</p><p>It is possible that their counterparts (right-handed neutrinos and left-handed antineutrinos) simply do not exist. If they do, their properties are substantially different from observable neutrinos and antineutrinos. It is theorized that they are either very heavy (on the order of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GUT_scale" title="GUT scale">GUT scale</a>&#8212;see <i><a href="/wiki/Seesaw_mechanism" title="Seesaw mechanism">Seesaw mechanism</a></i>), do not participate in weak interaction (so-called <i><a href="/wiki/Sterile_neutrino" title="Sterile neutrino">sterile neutrinos</a></i>), or both.\n</p><p>The existence of nonzero neutrino masses somewhat complicates the situation. Neutrinos are produced in weak interactions as chirality eigenstates. Chirality of a massive particle is not a constant of motion; helicity is, but the chirality operator does not share eigenstates with the helicity operator. Free neutrinos propagate as mixtures of left- and right-handed helicity states, with mixing amplitudes on the order of <i>m</i><sub>&#957;</sub>/<i>E</i>. This does not significantly affect the experiments, because neutrinos involved are nearly always ultrarelativistic, and thus mixing amplitudes are vanishingly small. Effectively, they travel so quickly and time passes so slowly in their rest-frames that they do not have enough time to change over any observable path. For example, most solar neutrinos have energies on the order of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6986160217648700000&#9824;</span>0.100&#160;MeV</span>&#8211;<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6987160217648700000&#9824;</span>1&#160;MeV</span>, so the fraction of neutrinos with "wrong" helicity among them cannot exceed <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6990100000000000000&#9824;</span>10<sup>&#8722;10</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="GSI_anomaly">GSI anomaly</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: GSI anomaly">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/GSI_anomaly" title="GSI anomaly">GSI anomaly</a></div>\n<p>An unexpected series of experimental results for the rate of decay of heavy <a href="/wiki/Highly_charged_ion" title="Highly charged ion">highly charged</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a> <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> circulating in a <a href="/wiki/Storage_ring" title="Storage ring">storage ring</a> has provoked theoretical activity in an effort to find a convincing explanation. The rates of <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak</a> decay of two radioactive species with half lives of about 40 s and 200 s are found to have a significant <a href="/wiki/Oscillation" title="Oscillation">oscillatory</a> <a href="/wiki/Modulation" title="Modulation">modulation</a>, with a period of about 7 s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KienleBosch2013_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-KienleBosch2013-92">[89]</a></sup>\nThe observed phenomenon is known as the <a href="/wiki/GSI_anomaly" title="GSI anomaly">GSI anomaly</a>, as the storage ring is a facility at the <a href="/wiki/GSI_Helmholtz_Centre_for_Heavy_Ion_Research" title="GSI Helmholtz Centre for Heavy Ion Research">GSI Helmholtz Centre for Heavy Ion Research</a> in <a href="/wiki/Darmstadt" title="Darmstadt">Darmstadt</a> <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>.  As the decay process produces an <a href="/wiki/Electron_neutrino" title="Electron neutrino">electron neutrino</a>, some of the proposed explanations for the observed oscillation rate invoke neutrino properties. Initial ideas related to flavour oscillation were met with skepticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giunti2009_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Giunti2009-93">[90]</a></sup> A more recent proposal involves mass differences between neutrino mass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eigenstates" title="Eigenstates">eigenstates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gal2016_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gal2016-94">[91]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Artificial">Artificial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Artificial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactor_neutrinos">Reactor neutrinos</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Reactor neutrinos">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">Nuclear reactors</a> are the major source of human-generated neutrinos. The majority of energy in a nuclear reactor is generated by fission (the four main fissile isotopes in nuclear reactors are <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">235</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>, <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">238</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>, <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">239</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Pu<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span> and <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Plutonium-241" title="Plutonium-241"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">241</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Pu<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>), the resultant neutron-rich daughter nuclides rapidly undergo additional <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decays</a>, each converting one neutron to a proton and an electron and releasing an electron antineutrino (<span class="nowrap"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> &#8594; <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>p<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#8722;</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">&#957;</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span></span>). Including these subsequent decays, the average nuclear fission releases about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6989320435297400000&#9824;</span>200&#160;MeV</span> of energy, of which roughly 95.5% is retained in the core as heat, and roughly 4.5% (or about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6988144195883830000&#9824;</span>9&#160;MeV</span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup> is radiated away as antineutrinos. For a typical nuclear reactor with a thermal power of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7009400000000000000&#9824;</span>4000&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt" title="Megawatt">MW</a></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[d]</a></sup> the total power production from fissioning atoms is actually <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7009418500000000000&#9824;</span>4185&#160;MW</span>, of which <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7008185000000000000&#9824;</span>185&#160;MW</span> is radiated away as antineutrino radiation and never appears in the engineering. This is to say, <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7008185000000000000&#9824;</span>185&#160;MW</span> of fission energy is <i>lost</i> from this reactor and does not appear as heat available to run turbines, since antineutrinos penetrate all building materials practically without interaction.\n</p><p>The antineutrino energy spectrum depends on the degree to which the fuel is burned (plutonium-239 fission antineutrinos on average have slightly more energy than those from uranium-235 fission), but in general, the <i>detectable</i> antineutrinos from fission have a peak energy between about 3.5 and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6987640870594800000&#9824;</span>4&#160;MeV</span>, with a maximum energy of about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6988160217648700000&#9824;</span>10&#160;MeV</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[93]</a></sup> There is no established experimental method to measure the flux of low-energy antineutrinos. Only antineutrinos with an energy above threshold of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6987288391767660000&#9824;</span>1.8&#160;MeV</span> can trigger <a href="/wiki/Inverse_beta_decay" title="Inverse beta decay">inverse beta decay</a> and thus be unambiguously identified (see <a href="#Detection">&#167;&#160;Detection</a> below). An estimated 3% of all antineutrinos from a nuclear reactor carry an energy above this threshold. Thus, an average nuclear power plant may generate over <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7020100000000000000&#9824;</span>10<sup>20</sup></span> antineutrinos per second above this threshold, but also a much larger number (97%/3% &#8776; 30 times this number) below the energy threshold, which cannot be seen with present detector technology. The ND280 detector has been proposed as a viable safeguard unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[94]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Accelerator_neutrinos">Accelerator neutrinos</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Accelerator neutrinos">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Some <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerators</a> have been used to make neutrino beams. The technique is to collide <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> with a fixed target, producing charged <a href="/wiki/Pion" title="Pion">pions</a> or <a href="/wiki/Kaon" title="Kaon">kaons</a>. These unstable particles are then magnetically focused into a long tunnel where they decay while in flight. Because of the <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_transformation" title="Lorentz transformation">relativistic boost</a> of the decaying particle, the neutrinos are produced as a beam rather than isotropically. Efforts to construct an accelerator facility where neutrinos are produced through <a href="/wiki/Muon" title="Muon">muon</a> decays are ongoing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[95]</a></sup> Such a setup is generally known as a <a href="/wiki/Neutrino_Factory" title="Neutrino Factory">"neutrino factory"</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_weapons">Nuclear weapons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Nuclear weapons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">Nuclear weapons</a> also produce very large quantities of neutrinos. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fred_Reines" title="Fred Reines">Fred Reines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clyde_Cowan" title="Clyde Cowan">Clyde Cowan</a> considered the detection of neutrinos from a bomb prior to their search for reactor neutrinos; a fission reactor was recommended as a better alternative by Los Alamos physics division leader J.M.B. Kellogg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[96]</a></sup> Fission weapons produce antineutrinos (from the fission process), and fusion weapons produce both neutrinos (from the fusion process) and antineutrinos (from the initiating fission explosion).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geologic">Geologic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Geologic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geoneutrino" title="Geoneutrino">Geoneutrino</a></div>\n<p>Neutrinos are produced together with the natural <a href="/wiki/Background_radiation" title="Background radiation">background radiation</a>. In particular, the decay chains of <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">238</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span> and <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thorium-232" title="Thorium-232"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">232</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Th<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span> isotopes, as well as<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>, include <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decays</a> which emit antineutrinos. These so-called geoneutrinos can provide valuable information on the Earth\'s interior. A first indication for geoneutrinos was found by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KamLAND" title="KamLAND">KamLAND</a> experiment in 2005, updated results have been presented by KamLAND<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[97]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Borexino" title="Borexino">Borexino</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[98]</a></sup> The main background in the geoneutrino measurements are the antineutrinos coming from reactors.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Proton_proton_cycle.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="340" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Proton_proton_cycle.svg/330px-Proton_proton_cycle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Proton_proton_cycle.svg/495px-Proton_proton_cycle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Proton_proton_cycle.svg/660px-Proton_proton_cycle.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Proton_proton_cycle.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Solar neutrinos (<a href="/wiki/Proton%E2%80%93proton_chain_reaction" title="Proton&#8211;proton chain reaction">proton&#8211;proton chain</a>) in the Standard Solar Model</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric">Atmospheric</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Atmospheric">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Atmospheric neutrinos result from the interaction of <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a> with atomic nuclei in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth\'s atmosphere">Earth\'s atmosphere</a>, creating showers of particles, many of which are unstable and produce neutrinos when they decay. A collaboration of particle physicists from <a href="/wiki/Tata_Institute_of_Fundamental_Research" title="Tata Institute of Fundamental Research">Tata Institute of Fundamental Research</a> (India), <a href="/wiki/Osaka_City_University" title="Osaka City University">Osaka City University</a> (Japan) and <a href="/wiki/Durham_University" title="Durham University">Durham University</a> (UK) recorded the first cosmic ray neutrino interaction in an underground laboratory in <a href="/wiki/Kolar_Gold_Fields" title="Kolar Gold Fields">Kolar Gold Fields</a> in India in 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[99]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar">Solar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Solar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Solar_neutrino" title="Solar neutrino">Solar neutrino</a></div>\n<p>Solar neutrinos originate from the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> powering the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and other stars.\nThe details of the operation of the Sun are explained by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Solar_Model" title="Standard Solar Model">Standard Solar Model</a>. In short: when four protons fuse to become one <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> nucleus, two of them have to convert into neutrons, and each such conversion releases one electron neutrino.\n</p><p>The Sun sends enormous numbers of neutrinos in all directions. Each second, about 65 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" title="1000000000 (number)">billion</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7010650000000000000&#9824;</span>6.5<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>10</sup></span>) solar neutrinos pass through every square centimeter on the part of the Earth orthogonal to the direction of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-J._Bahcall_et_al._2005_L85&#8211;L88_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-J._Bahcall_et_al._2005_L85&#8211;L88-12">[12]</a></sup> Since neutrinos are insignificantly absorbed by the mass of the Earth, the surface area on the side of the Earth opposite the Sun receives about the same number of neutrinos as the side facing the Sun.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supernovae">Supernovae</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Supernovae">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Supernova_Early_Warning_System" title="Supernova Early Warning System">Supernova Early Warning System</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diffuse_supernova_neutrino_background" title="Diffuse supernova neutrino background">Diffuse supernova neutrino background</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Supernova-1987a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1190" data-file-width="1309" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Supernova-1987a.jpg/220px-Supernova-1987a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Supernova-1987a.jpg/330px-Supernova-1987a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Supernova-1987a.jpg/440px-Supernova-1987a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Supernova-1987a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supernova_1987a" title="Supernova 1987a">SN 1987A</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1966, Colgate and White<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S._A._Colgate_and_R._H._White_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-S._A._Colgate_and_R._H._White-104">[100]</a></sup>\ncalculated that neutrinos carry away most of the gravitational energy released by the collapse of massive stars, events now categorized as <a href="/wiki/Type_Ib_and_Ic_supernovae" title="Type Ib and Ic supernovae">Type&#160;Ib and Ic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova" title="Type II supernova">Type&#160;II</a> <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a>. When such stars collapse, matter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Densities" title="Densities">densities</a> at the core become so high (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7017100000000000000&#9824;</span>10<sup>17</sup>&#160;kg/m<sup>3</sup></span>) that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degeneracy_pressure" title="Degeneracy pressure">degeneracy</a> of electrons is not enough to prevent protons and electrons from combining to form a neutron and an electron neutrino. A second and more important neutrino source is the thermal energy (100&#160;billion&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvins</a>) of the newly formed neutron core, which is dissipated via the formation of neutrino&#8211;antineutrino pairs of all flavors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mannbook_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mannbook-105">[101]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Colgate and White\'s theory of supernova neutrino production was confirmed in 1987, when neutrinos from <a href="/wiki/SN_1987A" title="SN 1987A">Supernova&#160;1987A</a> were detected. The water-based detectors <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kamiokande_II" title="Kamiokande II">Kamiokande II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irvine%E2%80%93Michigan%E2%80%93Brookhaven_(detector)" title="Irvine&#8211;Michigan&#8211;Brookhaven (detector)">IMB</a> detected 11 and 8&#160;antineutrinos (<a href="/wiki/Lepton_number" title="Lepton number">lepton number</a>&#160;=&#160;&#8722;1) of thermal origin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mannbook_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mannbook-105">[101]</a></sup> respectively, while the scintillator-based <a href="/wiki/Baksan_Neutrino_Observatory" title="Baksan Neutrino Observatory">Baksan</a> detector found 5&#160;neutrinos (<a href="/wiki/Lepton_number" title="Lepton number">lepton number</a>&#160;=&#160;+1) of either thermal or electron-capture origin, in a burst less than 13&#160;seconds long. The neutrino signal from the supernova arrived at earth several hours before the arrival of the first electromagnetic radiation, as expected from the evident fact that the latter emerges along with the shock wave. The exceptionally feeble interaction with normal matter allowed the neutrinos to pass through the churning mass of the exploding star, while the electromagnetic photons were slowed.\n</p><p>Because neutrinos interact so little with matter, it is thought that a supernova\'s neutrino emissions carry information about the innermost regions of the explosion. Much of the <i>visible</i> light comes from the decay of radioactive elements produced by the supernova shock wave, and even light from the explosion itself is scattered by dense and turbulent gases, and thus delayed. The neutrino burst is expected to reach Earth before any electromagnetic waves, including visible light, gamma rays, or radio waves. The exact time delay of the electromagnetic waves\' arrivals depends on the velocity of the shock wave and on the thickness of the outer layer of the star. For a Type&#160;II supernova, astronomers expect the neutrino flood to be released seconds after the stellar core collapse, while the first electromagnetic signal may emerge hours later, after the explosion shock wave has had time to reach the surface of the star. The <a href="/wiki/Supernova_Early_Warning_System" title="Supernova Early Warning System">Supernova Early Warning System</a> project uses a network of neutrino detectors to monitor the sky for candidate supernova events; the neutrino signal will provide a useful advance warning of a star exploding in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>.\n</p><p>Although neutrinos pass through the outer gases of a supernova without scattering, they provide information about the deeper supernova core with evidence that here, even neutrinos scatter to a significant extent. In a supernova core the densities are those of a neutron star (which is expected to be formed in this type of supernova),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[102]</a></sup> becoming large enough to influence the duration of the neutrino signal by delaying some neutrinos. The 13&#160;second-long neutrino signal from SN&#160;1987A lasted far longer than it would take for unimpeded neutrinos to cross through the neutrino-generating core of a supernova, expected to be only 3200 kilometers in diameter for SN&#160;1987A.\n</p><p>The number of neutrinos counted was also consistent with a total neutrino energy of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7046220000000000000&#9824;</span>2.2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>46</sup>&#160;joules</span>, which was estimated to be nearly all of the total energy of the supernova.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[103]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For an average supernova, approximately 10<sup>+57</sup> (an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octodecillion" title="Octodecillion">octodecillion</a>) neutrinos are released, but the actual number detected at a terrestrial detector <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle N}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>N</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle N}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="N" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f5e3890c981ae85503089652feb48b191b57aae3" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.064ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> will be far smaller, at the level of\n</p><p><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle N\\sim 10^{4}\\left({\\frac {M}{25\\mathrm {kton} }}\\right)\\left({\\frac {d}{10\\mathrm {kpc} }}\\right)^{-2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>N</mi>\n<mo>&#8764;<!-- &#8764; --></mo>\n<msup>\n<mn>10</mn>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>4</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>M</mi>\n<mrow>\n<mn>25</mn>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">k</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">t</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">o</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">n</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mrow>\n<mn>10</mn>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">k</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">p</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">c</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle N\\sim 10^{4}\\left({\\frac {M}{25\\mathrm {kton} }}\\right)\\left({\\frac {d}{10\\mathrm {kpc} }}\\right)^{-2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle N\\sim 10^{4}\\left({\\frac {M}{25\\mathrm {kton} }}\\right)\\left({\\frac {d}{10\\mathrm {kpc} }}\\right)^{-2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9e2f4cb7b3d22e8dcf300cbb573c7a7bb16f33b4" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:32.952ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span>,\n</p><p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle M}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>M</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle M}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="M" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f82cade9898ced02fdd08712e5f0c0151758a0dd" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.442ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the mass of the detector (with e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super_Kamiokande" title="Super Kamiokande">Super Kamiokande</a> having a mass of 50&#160;kton) and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle d}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>d</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle d}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="d" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e85ff03cbe0c7341af6b982e47e9f90d235c66ab" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.216ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the distance to the supernova.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[104]</a></sup> Hence in practice it will only be possible to detect neutrino bursts from supernovae within or nearby the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> (our own galaxy). In addition to the detection of neutrinos from individual supernovae, it should also be possible to detect the <a href="/wiki/Diffuse_supernova_neutrino_background" title="Diffuse supernova neutrino background">diffuse supernova neutrino background</a>, which originates from all supernovae in the Universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[105]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supernova_remnants">Supernova remnants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Supernova remnants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The energy of supernova neutrinos ranges from a few to several tens of MeV. The sites where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_rays" title="Cosmic rays">cosmic rays</a> are accelerated are expected to produce neutrinos that are at least one million times more energetic, produced from turbulent gaseous environments left over by supernova explosions: the <a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">supernova remnants</a>. The origin of the cosmic rays was attributed to supernovas by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Baade" title="Walter Baade">Walter Baade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Zwicky" title="Fritz Zwicky">Fritz Zwicky</a>; this hypothesis was refined by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vitaly_L._Ginzburg" title="Vitaly L. Ginzburg">Vitaly L. Ginzburg</a> and Sergei I. Syrovatsky who attributed the origin to supernova remnants, and supported their claim by the crucial remark, that the cosmic ray losses of the Milky Way is compensated, if the efficiency of acceleration in supernova remnants is about 10 percent. Ginzburg and Syrovatskii\'s hypothesis is supported by the specific mechanism of "shock wave acceleration" happening in supernova remnants, which is consistent with the original theoretical picture drawn by <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Fermi" title="Enrico Fermi">Enrico Fermi</a>, and is receiving support from observational data. The very-high-energy neutrinos are still to be seen, but this branch of neutrino astronomy is just in its infancy. The main existing or forthcoming experiments that aim at observing very-high-energy neutrinos from our galaxy are <a href="/wiki/Baikal_Deep_Underwater_Neutrino_Telescope" title="Baikal Deep Underwater Neutrino Telescope">Baikal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Muon_And_Neutrino_Detector_Array" title="Antarctic Muon And Neutrino Detector Array">AMANDA</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IceCube" title="IceCube">IceCube</a>, <a href="/wiki/ANTARES_(telescope)" title="ANTARES (telescope)">ANTARES</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutrino_Mediterranean_Observatory" title="Neutrino Mediterranean Observatory">NEMO</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestor_Project" title="Nestor Project">Nestor</a>. Related information is provided by <a href="/wiki/Ultra-high-energy_gamma_ray" title="Ultra-high-energy gamma ray">very-high-energy gamma ray</a> observatories, such as <a href="/wiki/VERITAS" title="VERITAS">VERITAS</a>, <a href="/wiki/High_Energy_Stereoscopic_System" title="High Energy Stereoscopic System">HESS</a> and <a href="/wiki/MAGIC_(telescope)" title="MAGIC (telescope)">MAGIC</a>. Indeed, the collisions of cosmic rays are supposed to produce charged pions, whose decay give the neutrinos, and also neutral pions, whose decay give gamma rays: the environment of a supernova remnant is transparent to both types of radiation.\n</p><p>Still-higher-energy neutrinos, resulting from the interactions of extragalactic cosmic rays, could be observed with the <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Auger_Observatory" title="Pierre Auger Observatory">Pierre Auger Observatory</a> or with the dedicated experiment named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ANtarctic_Impulse_Transient_Antenna" title="ANtarctic Impulse Transient Antenna">ANITA</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Big_Bang">Big Bang</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Big Bang">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_neutrino_background" title="Cosmic neutrino background">Cosmic neutrino background</a></div>\n<p>It is thought that, just like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">cosmic microwave background radiation</a> left over from the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, there is a background of low-energy neutrinos in our Universe. In the 1980s it was proposed that these may be the explanation for the <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> thought to exist in the universe. Neutrinos have one important advantage over most other dark matter candidates: it is known that they exist. This idea also has serious problems.\n</p><p>From particle experiments, it is known that neutrinos are very light. This means that they easily move at speeds close to the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>. For this reason, dark matter made from neutrinos is termed "<a href="/wiki/Hot_dark_matter" title="Hot dark matter">hot dark matter</a>". The problem is that being fast moving, the neutrinos would tend to have spread out evenly in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> before cosmological expansion made them cold enough to congregate in clumps. This would cause the part of <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> made of neutrinos to be smeared out and unable to cause the large <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galactic</a> structures that we see.\n</p><p>These same galaxies and <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_groups_and_clusters" title="Galaxy groups and clusters">groups of galaxies</a> appear to be surrounded by dark matter that is not fast enough to escape from those galaxies. Presumably this matter provided the gravitational nucleus for <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_formation_and_evolution" title="Galaxy formation and evolution">formation</a>. This implies that neutrinos cannot make up a significant part of the total amount of dark matter.\n</p><p>From cosmological arguments, relic background neutrinos are estimated to have density of 56 of each type per cubic centimeter and temperature <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000190000000000000&#9824;</span>1.9&#160;K</span> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6977272370002790000&#9824;</span>1.7<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>&#8722;4</sup>&#160;eV</span>) if they are massless, much colder if their mass exceeds <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6978160217648700000&#9824;</span>0.001&#160;eV</span>. Although their density is quite high, they have not yet been observed in the laboratory, as their energy is below thresholds of most detection methods, and due to extremely low neutrino interaction cross-sections at sub-eV energies. In contrast, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boron-8" title="Boron-8">boron-8</a> solar neutrinos&#8212;which are emitted with a higher energy&#8212;have been detected definitively despite having a space density that is lower than that of relic neutrinos by some 6 orders of magnitude.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Detection">Detection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Detection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neutrino_detector" title="Neutrino detector">Neutrino detector</a></div>\n<p>Neutrinos cannot be detected directly, because they do not ionize the materials they are passing through (they do not carry electric charge and other proposed effects, like the MSW effect, do not produce traceable radiation). A unique reaction to identify antineutrinos, sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Inverse_beta_decay" title="Inverse beta decay">inverse beta decay</a>, as applied by Reines and Cowan (see below), requires a very large detector to detect a significant number of neutrinos. All detection methods require the neutrinos to carry a minimum threshold energy. So far, there is no detection method for low-energy neutrinos, in the sense that potential neutrino interactions (for example by the MSW effect) cannot be uniquely distinguished from other causes. Neutrino detectors are often built underground to isolate the detector from <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a> and other background radiation.\n</p><p>Antineutrinos were first detected in the 1950s near a nuclear reactor. <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Reines" title="Frederick Reines">Reines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clyde_Cowan" title="Clyde Cowan">Cowan</a> used two targets containing a solution of <a href="/wiki/Cadmium_chloride" title="Cadmium chloride">cadmium chloride</a> in water. Two scintillation detectors were placed next to the cadmium targets. Antineutrinos with an energy above the threshold of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6987288391767660000&#9824;</span>1.8&#160;MeV</span> caused charged current interactions with the protons in the water, producing positrons and neutrons. This is very much like <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#946;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> decay, where energy is used to convert a proton into a neutron, a <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>) and an <a href="/wiki/Electron_neutrino" title="Electron neutrino">electron neutrino</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#957;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span>) is emitted:\n</p><p>From known <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#946;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> decay:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Energy + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>p<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> &#8594; <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#957;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span></dd></dl>\n<p>In the Cowan and Reines experiment, instead of an outgoing neutrino, you have an incoming antineutrino (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">&#957;</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span>) from a nuclear reactor:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Energy (&gt;<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6987288391767660000&#9824;</span>1.8&#160;MeV</span>) + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>p<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">&#957;</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span> &#8594; <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></dd></dl>\n<p>The resulting positron annihilation with electrons in the detector material created photons with an energy of about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6986801088243500000&#9824;</span>0.5&#160;MeV</span>. Pairs of photons in coincidence could be detected by the two scintillation detectors above and below the target. The neutrons were captured by cadmium nuclei resulting in gamma rays of about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6988128174118960000&#9824;</span>8&#160;MeV</span> that were detected a few microseconds after the photons from a positron annihilation event.\n</p><p>Since then, various detection methods have been used. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super_Kamiokande" title="Super Kamiokande">Super Kamiokande</a> is a large volume of water surrounded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photomultiplier_tube" title="Photomultiplier tube">photomultiplier tubes</a> that watch for the <a href="/wiki/Cherenkov_radiation" title="Cherenkov radiation">Cherenkov radiation</a> emitted when an incoming neutrino creates an <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> or <a href="/wiki/Muon" title="Muon">muon</a> in the water. The <a href="/wiki/Sudbury_Neutrino_Observatory" title="Sudbury Neutrino Observatory">Sudbury Neutrino Observatory</a> is similar, but uses <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">heavy water</a> as the detecting medium, which uses the same effects, but also allows the additional reaction any-flavor neutrino photo-dissociation of deuterium, resulting in a free neutron which is then detected from gamma radiation after chlorine-capture. Other detectors have consisted of large volumes of <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a> which are periodically checked for excesses of <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>, respectively, which are created by electron-neutrinos interacting with the original substance. <a href="/wiki/MINOS" title="MINOS">MINOS</a> used a solid plastic <a href="/wiki/Scintillator" title="Scintillator">scintillator</a> coupled to photomultiplier tubes, while <a href="/wiki/Borexino" title="Borexino">Borexino</a> uses a liquid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudocumene" title="Pseudocumene">pseudocumene</a> scintillator also watched by photomultiplier tubes and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NO%CE%BDA" title="NO&#957;A">NO&#957;A</a> detector uses liquid scintillator watched by <a href="/wiki/Avalanche_photodiode" title="Avalanche photodiode">avalanche photodiodes</a>. The <a href="/wiki/IceCube_Neutrino_Observatory" title="IceCube Neutrino Observatory">IceCube Neutrino Observatory</a> uses <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7009100000000000000&#9824;</span>1&#160;km<sup>3</sup></span> of the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">Antarctic ice sheet</a> near the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_pole" title="South pole">south pole</a> with photomultiplier tubes distributed throughout the volume.\nThe University of Liverpool ND280 detector employs the novel use of gadolinium encased light detectors in a temperature controlled magnetic field capturing double light pulse events. The T2K experiment developed the technology and practical experiments were successful in both Japan and at Wylfa power station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[106]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_interest">Scientific interest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutrino&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Scientific interest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Neutrinos\' low mass and neutral charge mean they interact exceedingly weakly with other particles and fields. This feature of weak interaction interests scientists because it means neutrinos can be used to probe environments that other radiation (such as light or radio waves) cannot penetrate.\n</p><p>Using neutrinos as a probe was first proposed in the mid-20th century as a way to detect conditions at the core of the Sun. The solar core cannot be imaged directly because electromagnetic radiation (such as light) is diffused by the great amount and density of matter surrounding the core. On the other hand, neutrinos pass through the Sun with few interactions.
